     <p>The <b>University of Virginia</b> (often abbreviated as <b>UVA</b>, <b>UVa</b>, or <b>Virginia</b>) is a <a href="/wiki/Public_university" title="Public university">public</a> <a href="/wiki/Research_university" title="Research university" class="mw-redirect">research university</a> in <a href="/wiki/Charlottesville" title="Charlottesville" class="mw-redirect">Charlottesville</a>, <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>. It was conceived and designed by <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">U.S. President</a> <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>, and established in 1819. UVA's initial <a href="/wiki/Board_of_Visitors" title="Board of Visitors" class="mw-redirect">Board of Visitors</a> included former Presidents of the United States <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a>, and <a href="/wiki/James_Monroe" title="James Monroe">James Monroe</a>. Monroe owned the initial site of the University, which was mostly farmland. His law office and farmhouse are now the site of <a href="/wiki/Brown_College_at_Monroe_Hill" title="Brown College at Monroe Hill">Brown College at Monroe Hill</a>, a residential college at UVA.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>UVA is one of the eight original <a href="/wiki/Public_Ivy" title="Public Ivy">Public Ivy</a> universities, and it is the only university campus in the United States that is designated a <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a> by <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> In the 2013 edition of <a href="/wiki/U.S._News_and_World_Report" title="U.S. News and World Report" class="mw-redirect">U.S. News and World Report</a>'s National University Rankings, the school was listed as America's 2nd best public university; tied with <a href="/wiki/UCLA" title="UCLA" class="mw-redirect">UCLA</a> and surpassed only by <a href="/wiki/UC_Berkeley" title="UC Berkeley" class="mw-redirect">UC Berkeley</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <table id="toc" class="toc">

     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Virginia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1800s">1800s</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Virginia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: 1800s">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Founding">Founding</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Virginia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Founding">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>On January 18, 1800, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Vice_President_of_the_United_States" title="Vice President of the United States">Vice President of the United States</a>, alluded to plans for a new college in a letter written to British scientist <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Priestley" title="Joseph Priestley">Joseph Priestley</a>: &quot;We wish to establish in the upper country of Virginia, and more centrally for the State, a University on a plan so broad and liberal and modern, as to be worth patronizing with the public support, and be a temptation to the youth of other States to come and drink of the cup of knowledge and fraternize with us.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-wish_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wish-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 1802, then serving as <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President of the United States</a>, Jefferson wrote to artist <a href="/wiki/Charles_Willson_Peale" title="Charles Willson Peale">Charles Willson Peale</a> that his concept of the new university would be &quot;on the most extensive and liberal scale that our circumstances would call for and our faculties meet.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-Peale_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peale-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> Virginia was already home to <a href="/wiki/The_College_of_William_%26_Mary" title="The College of William &amp; Mary" class="mw-redirect">The College of William &amp; Mary</a>, but Jefferson lost confidence in his alma mater, partly because of its religious stances and lack of courses in the sciences.<sup id="cite_ref-bias_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bias-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> Although Jefferson flourished under the tutelage of <a href="/wiki/College_of_William_%26_Mary" title="College of William &amp; Mary">College of William &amp; Mary</a> professors <a href="/wiki/William_Small" title="William Small">William Small</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_Wythe" title="George Wythe">George Wythe</a>, his concerns with the College became great enough by 1800 that he wrote: &quot;We have in that State, a college just well enough endowed to draw out the miserable existence to which a miserable constitution has doomed it.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-bias2_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bias2-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> Thus, he began planning a university more aligned with his educational ideals.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:James_Monroe_White_House_portrait_1819.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/James_Monroe_White_House_portrait_1819.gif/150px-James_Monroe_White_House_portrait_1819.gif" width="150" height="181" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/James_Monroe_White_House_portrait_1819.gif/225px-James_Monroe_White_House_portrait_1819.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/James_Monroe_White_House_portrait_1819.gif/300px-James_Monroe_White_House_portrait_1819.gif 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:James_Monroe_White_House_portrait_1819.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The University of Virginia stands on land once owned by President 
        <a href="/wiki/James_Monroe" title="James Monroe">James Monroe</a>, who sold the land when he moved into the 
        <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a>.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The University of Virginia stands on land purchased in 1788 by an <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a> veteran (and eventual fifth <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President of the United States</a>), <a href="/wiki/James_Monroe" title="James Monroe">James Monroe</a>. The farmland just outside <a href="/wiki/Charlottesville,_Virginia" title="Charlottesville, Virginia">Charlottesville</a> was purchased from Monroe by the Board of Visitors as <i>Central College</i> in 1817; Monroe was beginning the first of two terms in the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a>. Guided by Jefferson, the school laid its first building's cornerstone in late 1817, and the Commonwealth of Virginia chartered the new university on January 25, 1819.</p> 
     <p>In the presence of <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_du_Motier,_marquis_de_La_Fayette" title="Gilbert du Motier, marquis de La Fayette" class="mw-redirect">Marquis de Lafayette</a> toasted Jefferson as &quot;father&quot; of the University of Virginia at the school's inaugural banquet in 1824. The University's first classes met in March 1825. Other universities of the day allowed only three choices of specialization: Medicine, Law, and Religion. Under Jefferson's guidance, the University of Virginia became the first in the United States to allow specializations in such diverse fields as <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">Astronomy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Architecture" title="Architecture">Architecture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Botany" title="Botany">Botany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">Philosophy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Political_Science" title="Political Science" class="mw-redirect">Political Science</a>. Jefferson explained, &quot;This institution will be based on the illimitable freedom of the human mind. For here we are not afraid to follow truth wherever it may lead, nor to tolerate any error so long as reason is left free to combat it.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-Reason_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reason-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Chapel_at_the_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4250.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Chapel_at_the_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4250.JPG/200px-Chapel_at_the_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4250.JPG" width="200" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Chapel_at_the_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4250.JPG/300px-Chapel_at_the_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4250.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Chapel_at_the_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4250.JPG/400px-Chapel_at_the_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4250.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Chapel_at_the_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4250.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The non-denominational chapel near the Rotunda of the university was established in 1890, beyond the original scope of Jefferson's plans.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Another_view_of_chapel_at_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4255.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Another_view_of_chapel_at_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4255.JPG/200px-Another_view_of_chapel_at_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4255.JPG" width="200" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Another_view_of_chapel_at_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4255.JPG/300px-Another_view_of_chapel_at_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4255.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Another_view_of_chapel_at_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4255.JPG/400px-Another_view_of_chapel_at_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4255.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Another_view_of_chapel_at_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4255.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Inside the UVA Chapel
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>An even more controversial direction was taken for the new university based on a daring vision that higher education should be completely separated from religious doctrine. One of the largest construction projects in North America up to that time, the new Grounds were centered upon a library (then housed in <a href="/wiki/The_Rotunda_(University_of_Virginia)" title="The Rotunda (University of Virginia)">the Rotunda</a>) rather than a church ? further distinguishing it from peer universities of the United States, most of which were still primarily functioning as seminaries for one particular religion or another.<sup id="cite_ref-sem_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sem-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> Jefferson even went so far as to ban the teaching of Theology altogether. In a letter to <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Cooper_(US_politician)" title="Thomas Cooper (US politician)" class="mw-redirect">Thomas Cooper</a> in October 1814, Jefferson stated, &quot;a professorship of theology should have no place in our institution&quot; and, true to form, the University has never had a Divinity school; it was established independent of any religious sect. Replacing the then-standard specialization in Religion, the University undertook groundbreaking specializations in scientific subjects such as Astronomy and Botany. (However, today UVA does maintain a strong Religious Studies department. A non-denominational chapel, notably absent from Jefferson's original plans, was constructed in 1890.)</p> 
     <p>Jefferson was intimately involved in the university, hosting Sunday dinners at his <a href="/wiki/Monticello" title="Monticello">Monticello</a> home for faculty and students until his death. So taken with the import of what he viewed the University's foundations and potential to be, and counting it amongst his greatest accomplishments, Jefferson insisted his grave mention only his status as author of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">Declaration of Independence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Statute_for_Religious_Freedom" title="Virginia Statute for Religious Freedom">Virginia Statute for Religious Freedom</a>, and father of the University of Virginia. Thus, he eschewed mention of his presidency and national accomplishments in favor of being remembered for the newly established university.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Lawn.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Lawn.jpg/250px-Lawn.jpg" width="250" height="356" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Lawn.jpg/375px-Lawn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fd/Lawn.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Lawn.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/The_Lawn" title="The Lawn">The Lawn</a> during winter, with tracks through the snow. At center is the 
        <a href="/wiki/The_Rotunda_(University_of_Virginia)" title="The Rotunda (University of Virginia)">Rotunda</a>, which was the original library building at the head of Jefferson's Academical Village.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>In 1826, the nation's fourth president, <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a>, became Rector of the University of Virginia, at the same time America's fifth President <a href="/wiki/James_Monroe" title="James Monroe">James Monroe</a> made his home on the Grounds (at <a href="/wiki/Brown_College_at_Monroe_Hill" title="Brown College at Monroe Hill">Monroe Hill</a>) and was a member of the Board of Visitors. Both former Presidents stayed at the university until their deaths in the 1830s.</p> 
     <p>The same year, 1826, poet <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Allan_Poe" title="Edgar Allan Poe">Edgar Allan Poe</a> enrolled at the university, where he excelled in Latin.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Raven_Society" title="Raven Society">Raven Society</a>, an organization named after Poe's most famous poem, continues to maintain 13 West Range, the room Poe inhabited during the single semester he attended the University (he left because of financial difficulties).<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Virginia_School_of_Engineering_and_Applied_Science" title="University of Virginia School of Engineering and Applied Science">School of Engineering and Applied Science</a> opened in 1836, making it the first engineering school in the United States to be attached to a comprehensive university.</p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_American_Civil_War">The American Civil War</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Virginia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: The American Civil War">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>At the onset of the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a> (commonly referred to in the South as the <a href="/wiki/Naming_the_American_Civil_War#War_Between_the_States" title="Naming the American Civil War">War Between the States</a>), the University of Virginia was the largest in the <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">Southern United States</a> and second nationwide only to <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a> in its scope.<sup id="cite_ref-1860size_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1860size-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> Unlike many other colleges in the South, the university was kept open throughout the conflict, an especially remarkable feat with its home state being the site of more battles than any other. In March 1865, Union General <a href="/wiki/George_Armstrong_Custer" title="George Armstrong Custer">George Armstrong Custer</a> marched troops into Charlottesville, whereupon faculty and community leaders convinced him to spare the university. Though <a href="/wiki/Union_Army" title="Union Army">Union troops</a> camped on the Lawn and damaged many of the Pavilions, Custer's men left four days later without bloodshed and the university was able to return to its educational routines.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Thomas-Jefferson.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Thomas-Jefferson.jpg/200px-Thomas-Jefferson.jpg" width="200" height="275" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Thomas-Jefferson.jpg/300px-Thomas-Jefferson.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Thomas-Jefferson.jpg/400px-Thomas-Jefferson.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Thomas-Jefferson.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Father of the University of Virginia, 
        <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> was the first and only 
        <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President of the United States</a> to found an institution of higher learning.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1900s">1900s</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Virginia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1900s">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_university.27s_first_president">The university's first president</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Virginia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: The university's first president">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>Jefferson, ever the skeptic of central authority and bureaucracy, had originally decided that the University of Virginia would have no President. Rather, this power was to be shared by a Rector and a <a href="/wiki/Board_of_Visitors" title="Board of Visitors" class="mw-redirect">Board of Visitors</a>. As the 19th century waned, it became obvious this cumbersome arrangement was incapable of adequately handling the many administrative and fundraising tasks that had become necessary to support the growing University.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 1904, <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Alderman" title="Edwin Alderman">Edwin Alderman</a> resigned as President of <a href="/wiki/Tulane_University" title="Tulane University">Tulane University</a> to take the same position at the University of Virginia. As the university's first president, he embarked on a number of reforms for both the university and the state of Virginia's public educational systems in general. A reform specific to the University of Virginia was one of the first school-sponsored <a href="/wiki/Financial_aid_(educational_expenses)" title="Financial aid (educational expenses)" class="mw-redirect">financial aid</a> programs in all of higher learning<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and, though primitive by today's standards, it included a loan provision for needy men who were unable to pay.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Initially controversial and opposed by many at what had become a very traditional school, Alderman's progressive ideas stood the test of time. He remains the longest-tenured president in the university's history, having served for twenty-six years until his death in 1931. Alderman Library is named in his honor.</p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_II">World War II</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Virginia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: World War II">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>During <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, Virginia was one of 131 colleges and universities nationally that took part in the <a href="/wiki/V-12_Navy_College_Training_Program" title="V-12 Navy College Training Program">V-12 Navy College Training Program</a> which offered students a path to a Navy commission.<sup id="cite_ref-mercer-v-12_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mercer-v-12-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="William_Faulkner">William Faulkner</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Virginia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: William Faulkner">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>Pulitzer and Nobel Prize winner <a href="/wiki/William_Faulkner" title="William Faulkner">William Faulkner</a> became a writer-in-residence at the university in 1957, keeping open office hours until his death in 1962. He was named a Professor of English and had the title of &quot;Consultant on American Literature to the Alderman Library&quot;. Faulkner donated a large collection of his manuscripts, typescripts, and correspondence to the William Faulkner Foundation, which he created, and the foundation in turn donated the manuscripts to the library upon his death. These and many further Faulkner-related materials reside in the <a href="/wiki/Albert_and_Shirley_Small_Special_Collections_Library" title="Albert and Shirley Small Special Collections Library">Albert and Shirley Small Special Collections Library</a> today.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Desegregation_and_the_admission_of_women">Desegregation and the admission of women</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Virginia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Desegregation and the admission of women">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>The University of Virginia began the process of integration even before the 1954 <a href="/wiki/Brown_v._Board_of_Education" title="Brown v. Board of Education">Brown v. Board of Education</a> decision mandated school desegregation for all grade levels, when Gregory Swanson sued to gain entrance into the university's law school in 1950.<sup id="cite_ref-Road_to_Desegregation_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Road_to_Desegregation-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> Following his successful lawsuit, a handful of black graduate and professional students were admitted during the 1950s, though no black undergraduates were admitted until 1955, and UVA like other southern schools continued to resist full integration until well into the 1960s.<sup id="cite_ref-Road_to_Desegregation_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Road_to_Desegregation-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The university first admitted a few selected women to graduate studies in the late 1890s and to certain programs such as nursing and education in the 1920s and 1930s.<sup id="cite_ref-Breaking_and_Making_Tradition_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Breaking_and_Making_Tradition-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1944, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Mary_Washington" title="University of Mary Washington">Mary Washington College</a> in <a href="/wiki/Fredericksburg,_Virginia" title="Fredericksburg, Virginia">Fredericksburg, Virginia</a> became the Women's Undergraduate Arts and Sciences Division of the University of Virginia. Until that time, the University of Virginia had not admitted women as <a href="/wiki/Undergraduate" title="Undergraduate" class="mw-redirect">undergraduates</a>. In 1970, the Charlottesville campus became fully co-educational, and in 1972 Mary Washington became an independent state university.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> Women were officially allowed to matriculate at UVA in 1970 after a court suit prompted the Board of Visitors to adopt full coeducation over a two-year period. In 1970, the first class of 450 undergraduate women entered UVA (39 percent), while the number of men admitted remained constant. By 2003 women comprised 55 percent of the undergraduate student body.<sup id="cite_ref-Breaking_and_Making_Tradition_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Breaking_and_Making_Tradition-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2000s">2000s</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Virginia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: 2000s">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Decline_in_state_support">Decline in state support</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Virginia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Decline in state support">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>In 2004, resulting from a stark decrease in state support, the University of Virginia became the first public university in the United States to receive more of its funding from private sources than from the state with which it is associated. Thanks to a Charter initiative that passed the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_General_Assembly" title="Virginia General Assembly">Virginia General Assembly</a> and was signed into law by then-<a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Virginia" title="Governor of Virginia">Governor</a> <a href="/wiki/Mark_Warner" title="Mark Warner">Mark Warner</a> in 2005,<sup id="cite_ref-Virginia_restructuring_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Virginia_restructuring-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> the university ? and any other public universities in the state that choose to do so (currently <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Polytechnic_Institute_and_State_University" title="Virginia Polytechnic Institute and State University" class="mw-redirect">Virginia Tech</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_College_of_William_%26_Mary" title="The College of William &amp; Mary" class="mw-redirect">William &amp; Mary</a>) ? will have greater autonomy over its own affairs.<sup id="cite_ref-Virginia_restructuring_FAQ_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Virginia_restructuring_FAQ-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Jm4.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Jm4.gif/150px-Jm4.gif" width="150" height="221" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a7/Jm4.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a7/Jm4.gif 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Jm4.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> President 
        <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a> served as the second Rector of the University of Virginia until his death in 1836.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Also in 2004, at the 100th anniversary of Alderman becoming President, UVA announced the AccessUVa financial aid program. This program guarantees the University will meet 100% of a U.S. student's demonstrated need. It also provides low-income students (up to 200% of the poverty line ? as of 2009, about <a href="/wiki/Poverty_threshold#National_poverty_lines" title="Poverty threshold">$44,000</a> for a family of four) with full grants to cover all of their educational needs, and it caps the level of need-based loans for all other students. This program was the first to guarantee full grants to students of low-income families at any public university in the United States.</p> 
     <p>Today, minority students are particularly successful at the University of Virginia. According to the Fall 2005 issue of <i>Journal of Blacks in Higher Education</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> UVA &quot;has the highest <a href="/wiki/African_American" title="African American">black</a> student graduation rate of the <a href="/wiki/Public_Ivy" title="Public Ivy">Public Ivies</a> at 86 percent.&quot; The journal goes on to state that &quot;by far the most impressive is the University of Virginia with its high black student graduation rate and its small racial difference in graduation rates.&quot;</p> 
     <p>The University of Virginia, together with <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a> and <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton University</a>, ended its <a href="/wiki/Early_Decision" title="Early Decision" class="mw-redirect">Early Decision</a> and <a href="/wiki/Early_Action" title="Early Action" class="mw-redirect">Early Action</a> programs in September 2006, stating that such policies limit poor and <a href="/wiki/Middle_class" title="Middle class">middle class</a> students from competing on an equal footing with wealthier applicants.<sup id="cite_ref-EarlyDecision1_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EarlyDecision1-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EarlyDecision2_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EarlyDecision2-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> For its part, UVA noted that of 947 Early Decision acceptances for the Class of 2010, fewer than 20 of those students had applied for financial aid.<sup id="cite_ref-EarlyDecision3_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EarlyDecision3-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> Non-binding early action was instated for the first time for the class of 2016 as ?early action did not have the same impact on students of lower socioeconomic means because it is not binding.? <sup id="cite_ref-EarlyDecision5_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EarlyDecision5-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Removal_and_reinstatement_of_President_Sullivan">Removal and reinstatement of President Sullivan</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Virginia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Removal and reinstatement of President Sullivan">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>In 2010 the University welcomed <a href="/wiki/Teresa_A._Sullivan" title="Teresa A. Sullivan">Teresa A. Sullivan</a> as the University's first woman President.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> Two years later, during the Spring of 2012, the first woman Rector <a href="/wiki/Helen_Dragas" title="Helen Dragas">Helen Dragas</a> decided to remove President Sullivan. Instead of convening the Board of Visitors to discuss firing the President, Ms. Dragas secretly lobbied Board members in one-on-one phone calls, and then surprised President Sullivan in her office on June 8, 2012 with a demand for her resignation.<sup id="cite_ref-Anatomy_of_a_Campus_Coup_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anatomy_of_a_Campus_Coup-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> Rector Dragas convened a three member Executive Committee meeting to accept the forced resignation, and then required Sullivan's subordinates to report directly to the Rector's office, effectively removing President Sullivan from any remaining management role.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>President Sullivan in a subsequent New York Times interview said she does not know why she was removed.<sup id="cite_ref-Anatomy_of_a_Campus_Coup_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anatomy_of_a_Campus_Coup-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> Professor <a href="/wiki/Larry_Sabato" title="Larry Sabato">Larry Sabato</a> in the UVA Alumni Magazine called it &quot;a midnight knifing,&quot; a &quot;palace coup&quot; to be reversed by &quot;a grassroots rebellion.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The resignation elicited strong protests, including a faculty Senate vote of no confidence in the Board of Visitors and Rector Dragas,<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> and demands from the student government for an explanation for Sullivan's ouster.<sup id="cite_ref-StudentCouncilReaction_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StudentCouncilReaction-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> In the face of mounting pressure, including alumni threats to cease contributions,<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> and a mandate from Virginia Governor <a href="/wiki/Robert_McDonnell" title="Robert McDonnell" class="mw-redirect">Robert McDonnell</a> to resolve the issue or he would remove the entire Board,<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> the Board unanimously voted to reinstate Sullivan as president.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In December 2012 the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Association_of_Colleges_and_Schools" title="Southern Association of Colleges and Schools">Southern Association of Colleges and Schools</a> put UVA &quot;on warning&quot; that it risked losing its accreditation because President Sullivan's removal violated their standards for board governance and faculty role in decisionmaking.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Grounds_.28Campus.29">The Grounds (Campus)</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Virginia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: The Grounds (Campus)">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <div class="rellink boilerplate seealso">
      See also: 
      <a href="/wiki/The_Lawn" title="The Lawn">The Lawn</a>, 
      <a href="/wiki/The_Rotunda_(University_of_Virginia)" title="The Rotunda (University of Virginia)">The Rotunda (University of Virginia)</a>,&nbsp;and 
      <a href="/wiki/The_Range" title="The Range">The Range</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:UVA_Rotunda.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/UVA_Rotunda.jpg/150px-UVA_Rotunda.jpg" width="150" height="100" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/UVA_Rotunda.jpg/225px-UVA_Rotunda.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/UVA_Rotunda.jpg/300px-UVA_Rotunda.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:UVA_Rotunda.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The Rotunda today
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Throughout its history, the University of Virginia has won praise for its unique <a href="/wiki/Jeffersonian_architecture" title="Jeffersonian architecture">Jeffersonian architecture</a>. In January 1895, less than a year before the Great Rotunda Fire, <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> said that the design of the University of Virginia &quot;was incomparably the most ambitious and monumental architectural project that had or has yet been conceived in this century&quot;.<sup id="cite_ref-schuyler_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schuyler-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Bicentennial" title="United States Bicentennial">United States Bicentennial</a> issue of their <i>AIA Journal</i>, the <a href="/wiki/American_Institute_of_Architects" title="American Institute of Architects">American Institute of Architects</a> called it &quot;the proudest achievement of American architecture in the past 200 years&quot;.<sup id="cite_ref-aia_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aia-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> Today, the University of Virginia remains an architectural landmark and popular tourist destination.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:University_of_Virginia_Rotunda_1819_draft.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/University_of_Virginia_Rotunda_1819_draft.jpg/150px-University_of_Virginia_Rotunda_1819_draft.jpg" width="150" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/University_of_Virginia_Rotunda_1819_draft.jpg/225px-University_of_Virginia_Rotunda_1819_draft.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/University_of_Virginia_Rotunda_1819_draft.jpg/300px-University_of_Virginia_Rotunda_1819_draft.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:University_of_Virginia_Rotunda_1819_draft.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Elevation of The Rotunda drawn by 
        <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> in 1819
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Jefferson's original architectural design revolves around the &quot;Academical Village&quot;, and that name remains in use today to describe both the specific area of <a href="/wiki/The_Lawn" title="The Lawn">The Lawn</a>, a grand, terraced green space surrounded by residential and academic buildings, the gardens, <a href="/wiki/The_Range" title="The Range">The Range</a>, and the larger University surrounding it. The principal building of the design, <a href="/wiki/The_Rotunda_(University_of_Virginia)" title="The Rotunda (University of Virginia)">The Rotunda</a>, stands at the north end of the Lawn, and is the most recognizable symbol of the University. It is half the height and width of the <a href="/wiki/Pantheon,_Rome" title="Pantheon, Rome">Pantheon</a> in <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a>, which was the primary inspiration for the building. The Lawn and the Rotunda were the model for many similar designs of &quot;centralized green areas&quot; at universities across the country.</p> 
     <p>Most notably designed by inspiration of the Rotunda and Lawn are the expansive green spaces headed by Rotunda-like buildings built at <a href="/wiki/Duke_University" title="Duke University">Duke University</a> in 1892, <a href="/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_University" title="Johns Hopkins University">Johns Hopkins University</a> in 1902, <a href="/wiki/Rice_University" title="Rice University">Rice University</a> in 1910, Dallas Hall, the central building at <a href="/wiki/Southern_Methodist_University" title="Southern Methodist University">Southern Methodist University</a> (1912), Peabody College of <a href="/wiki/Vanderbilt_University" title="Vanderbilt University">Vanderbilt University</a> in 1915, the Green at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Delaware" title="University of Delaware">University of Delaware</a> in 1916, Killian Court at <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">MIT</a> in 1916 (which was coincidentally founded by <a href="/wiki/William_Barton_Rogers" title="William Barton Rogers">William Barton Rogers</a>, who immediately prior to founding MIT was a <a href="/wiki/Natural_philosophy" title="Natural philosophy">Natural Philosophy</a> professor at the University of Virginia for 19&nbsp;years), the &quot;Grand Auditorium&quot; of <a href="/wiki/Tsinghua_University" title="Tsinghua University">Tsinghua University</a> in <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a> built in 1917, the campus of <a href="/wiki/Yale_Divinity_School" title="Yale Divinity School">Yale Divinity School</a> (the Sterling Quad, 1932), and, indeed, the new grounds of the University's own <a href="/wiki/Darden_Graduate_School_of_Business_Administration" title="Darden Graduate School of Business Administration" class="mw-redirect">Darden School of Business</a> (designed by Robert A.M. Stern).</p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Serpentine_wall.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Serpentine_wall.jpg/150px-Serpentine_wall.jpg" width="150" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Serpentine_wall.jpg/225px-Serpentine_wall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Serpentine_wall.jpg/300px-Serpentine_wall.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Serpentine_wall.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> An example of the serpentine wall design
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Flanking both sides of the Rotunda and extending down the length of the Lawn are ten Pavilions interspersed with student rooms. Each has its own classical architectural style, as well as its own walled garden separated by Jeffersonian Serpentine walls. These walls are called &quot;serpentine&quot; because they run a sinusoidal course, one that lends strength to the wall and allows for the wall to be only one brick thick, one of many innovations by which Jefferson attempted to combine aesthetics with utility. <a href="/wiki/Frank_E._Grizzard,_Jr." title="Frank E. Grizzard, Jr.">Frank E. Grizzard, Jr.</a>, a former scholar at the University, has written the definitive book on the original academic buildings at the University.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Jefferson financed the building of the University through personal loans from James Monroe and General John Hartwell Cocke, II. Monroe, Cocke, and Jefferson each put up 1/3 of the money to procure the land and build the initial buildings. Gen. Cocke was a General in the War of 1812, a local plantation owner, and friend of Thomas Jefferson. He owned Bremo Plantation, located southwest of Charlottesville near where Bremo Bluff, Virginia, is today. These loans were never repaid by Jefferson.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:University_of_Virginia_Rotunda_fire_small.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/University_of_Virginia_Rotunda_fire_small.jpg/150px-University_of_Virginia_Rotunda_fire_small.jpg" width="150" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/University_of_Virginia_Rotunda_fire_small.jpg/225px-University_of_Virginia_Rotunda_fire_small.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0b/University_of_Virginia_Rotunda_fire_small.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:University_of_Virginia_Rotunda_fire_small.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The Great Rotunda Fire, 1895
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>On October 27, 1895, the Rotunda burned to a shell because of an electrical fire that started in the Rotunda Annex, a long multi-story structure built in 1853 to house additional classrooms. The electrical fire was no doubt assisted by the unfortunate help of overzealous faculty member <a href="/wiki/William_Holding_Echols" title="William Holding Echols">William &quot;Reddy&quot; Echols</a>, who attempted to save it by throwing roughly 100&nbsp;pounds (45&nbsp;kg) of <a href="/wiki/Dynamite" title="Dynamite">dynamite</a> into the main fire in the hopes that the blast would separate the burning Annex from Mr. Jefferson's own Rotunda. His last-ditch effort ultimately failed. (Perhaps ironically, one of the University's main honors student programs is named for him.) University officials swiftly approached celebrity architect <a href="/wiki/Stanford_White" title="Stanford White">Stanford White</a> to rebuild the Rotunda. White took the charge further, disregarding Mr. Jefferson's design and redesigning the Rotunda interior ? making it two floors instead of three, adding three buildings to the foot of the Lawn, and designing a President's House. He did omit rebuilding the Rotunda Annex, the remnants of which were used as fill and to create part of the modern-day Rotunda's northern-facing plaza. The classes formerly occupying the Annex were now moved to the South Lawn in White's new buildings.</p> 
     <p>The White buildings have the effect of closing off the sweeping perspective, as originally conceived by Jefferson, down the Lawn across open countryside toward the distant mountains. The White buildings at the foot of the Lawn effectively create a huge &quot;quadrangle&quot;, albeit one far grander than any traditional college quadrangle at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">University of Cambridge</a> or <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">University of Oxford</a>.</p> 
     <p>In concert with the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Bicentennial" title="United States Bicentennial">United States Bicentennial</a> in 1976, Stanford White's changes to the Rotunda were removed and the building was returned to Jefferson's original design. Renovated according to original sketches and historical photographs, a three-story Rotunda opened on Jefferson's birthday, April 13, 1976.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Rotunda_(University_of_Virginia)_-_dome-(reverse).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Rotunda_%28University_of_Virginia%29_-_dome-%28reverse%29.JPG/150px-Rotunda_%28University_of_Virginia%29_-_dome-%28reverse%29.JPG" width="150" height="113" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Rotunda_%28University_of_Virginia%29_-_dome-%28reverse%29.JPG/225px-Rotunda_%28University_of_Virginia%29_-_dome-%28reverse%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Rotunda_%28University_of_Virginia%29_-_dome-%28reverse%29.JPG/300px-Rotunda_%28University_of_Virginia%29_-_dome-%28reverse%29.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Rotunda_(University_of_Virginia)_-_dome-(reverse).JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Inside the Dome Room of the Rotunda
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Though student enrollment has grown well beyond the original Lawn facilities, the University further distinguishes itself by extending the original Academical Village ideal with two exclusively First-Year (freshman) living areas: The Old Dorms (Bonnycastle, Dabney, Echols, Emmet, Hancock, Humphreys, Kent, Lefevre, Metcalf, Page), located on <a href="/wiki/McCormick_Road_(UVA)" title="McCormick Road (UVA)" class="mw-redirect">McCormick Road</a>, and The New Dorms (Cauthen, Courtenay, Dunglison, Dunnington, Fitzhugh, Kellogg, Balz-Dobie, Watson-Webb, Woody), adjacent to <a href="/wiki/Scott_Stadium" title="Scott Stadium">Scott Stadium</a>, both situated wholly on Grounds and considered integral to establishing peer discourse. The common bonding experience proves such a fixture to the University experience, students often identify themselves by individual &quot;Old&quot; or &quot;New&quot; dormitory. First-Year living areas also include <a href="/wiki/Hereford_College" title="Hereford College">Hereford College</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Residential_College&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="International Residential College (page does not exist)">International Residential College</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Brown_College_at_Monroe_Hill" title="Brown College at Monroe Hill">Brown College at Monroe Hill</a>.</p> 
     <p>In 2001, <a href="/wiki/John_Kluge" title="John Kluge">John Kluge</a> donated 7,378 acres (29.86&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup>) of additional lands to the University. Kluge desired the core of the land to be developed by the University, and the surrounding land to be sold to fund an endowment supporting the core. A large part of the gift was soon sold to musician <a href="/wiki/Dave_Matthews" title="Dave Matthews">Dave Matthews</a>, of the <a href="/wiki/Dave_Matthews_Band" title="Dave Matthews Band">Dave Matthews Band</a>, to be utilized in an <a href="/wiki/Organic_farming" title="Organic farming">organic farming</a> project. It is unknown what the University will do with its &quot;core&quot; portion of the land.</p> 
     <p>The <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Department_of_Transportation" title="Virginia Department of Transportation">Virginia Department of Transportation</a> maintains the roads through the University grounds as <a href="/wiki/State_Route_302_(Virginia)" title="State Route 302 (Virginia)" class="mw-redirect">State Route 302</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The University, together with Jefferson's home at <a href="/wiki/Monticello" title="Monticello">Monticello</a>, is a <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a>, one of only three modern sites so listed in the <a href="/wiki/U.S._state" title="U.S. state">50 states</a>, the others being the <a href="/wiki/Statue_of_Liberty" title="Statue of Liberty">Statue of Liberty</a> and <a href="/wiki/Independence_Hall_(United_States)" title="Independence Hall (United States)" class="mw-redirect">Independence Hall</a>. It was the first collegiate campus worldwide to be awarded the designation.</p> 
     <p><br style="clear:both;" /></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_luminary_gatherings_and_events">Modern luminary gatherings and events</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Virginia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Modern luminary gatherings and events">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:227px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Robert-Ted-John-Kennedy.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Robert-Ted-John-Kennedy.jpg/225px-Robert-Ted-John-Kennedy.jpg" width="225" height="246" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Robert-Ted-John-Kennedy.jpg/338px-Robert-Ted-John-Kennedy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Robert-Ted-John-Kennedy.jpg/450px-Robert-Ted-John-Kennedy.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Robert-Ted-John-Kennedy.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The 
        <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_family" title="Kennedy family">Kennedy brothers</a> appeared at U.Va. in 1958 while John was preparing to run for President. 
        <a href="/wiki/Ted_Kennedy" title="Ted Kennedy">Ted</a> and 
        <a href="/wiki/Robert_F._Kennedy" title="Robert F. Kennedy">Robert F. Kennedy</a> were both educated by the 
        <a href="/wiki/University_of_Virginia_School_of_Law" title="University of Virginia School of Law">School of Law</a> after attending 
        <a href="/wiki/Harvard_College" title="Harvard College">Harvard College</a> as undergrads.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>On June 10, 1940, U.S. President <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Delano_Roosevelt" title="Franklin Delano Roosevelt" class="mw-redirect">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a> came to the University's Memorial Gymnasium to watch his son <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Delano_Roosevelt,_Jr." title="Franklin Delano Roosevelt, Jr.">Franklin D. Roosevelt, Jr.</a> graduate, and to give the commencement address. Instead, &quot;in this university founded by the first great American teacher of democracy&quot; he made his impromptu &quot;Stab in the Back&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-FDR_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FDR-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> speech denouncing the act of Italy joining beside <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a> to invade France on that day.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> (Graduation ceremonies are traditionally held on the Lawn, but rain had forced a move to &quot;Mem Gym&quot; for the Class of 1940.)</p> 
     <p>Nearly two decades later, in 1958, Senator <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> visited and spoke in the same space with brothers <a href="/wiki/Robert_Kennedy" title="Robert Kennedy" class="mw-redirect">Robert Kennedy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ted_Kennedy" title="Ted Kennedy">Ted Kennedy</a>, the latter of whom was managing JFK's 1958 Senatorial re-election campaign from his dormitory at the University of Virginia.</p> 
     <p>In the early 1960s, civil rights leaders <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King,_Jr." title="Martin Luther King, Jr.">Martin Luther King, Jr.</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_L._Farmer,_Jr." title="James L. Farmer, Jr.">James Farmer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aaron_Henry" title="Aaron Henry">Aaron Henry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bayard_Rustin" title="Bayard Rustin">Bayard Rustin</a>, and others spoke at the University under the sponsorship of the Virginia Council on Human Relations, a student organization which presented speakers on Grounds who opposed the state's prevailing policy of racial segregation. <a href="/wiki/John_Lewis_(American_politician)" title="John Lewis (American politician)" class="mw-redirect">John Lewis</a>, Chairman of the <a href="/wiki/Student_Nonviolent_Coordinating_Committee" title="Student Nonviolent Coordinating Committee">Student Nonviolent Coordinating Committee</a> spoke in 1965 while his head was still bandaged from a police beating he received leading the <a href="/wiki/Selma_to_Montgomery_marches#The_First_March.2C_.22Bloody_Sunday.22" title="Selma to Montgomery marches">first march from Selma</a>.</p> 
     <p>Martin Luther King, Jr.'s speech at Old Cabell Hall in 1963 met with no resistance of any kind among the student body or administration.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> He called for a doubling of black registrations at universities in the South. African-American enrollment has since been increased tenfold at the University. Five years later in 1968, when King was shot, the Rotunda flag was flown at <a href="/wiki/Half-mast" title="Half-mast">half-mast</a> and then-President of the University <a href="/wiki/Edgar_F._Shannon,_Jr." title="Edgar F. Shannon, Jr.">Edgar Shannon, Jr.</a> led a U.Va. memorial service in Cabell Hall. All classes at U.Va. were made optional during the service.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>To commemorate the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Bicentennial" title="United States Bicentennial">United States Bicentennial</a> in 1976, Britain's <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Elizabeth II of the United Kingdom" class="mw-redirect">Queen Elizabeth II</a> strolled the Lawn and lunched in the Dome Room of the Rotunda, one of five American sites she publicly visited.</p> 
     <p>The <a href="/wiki/Tenzin_Gyatso" title="Tenzin Gyatso" class="mw-redirect">Dalai Lama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Archbishop" title="Archbishop">Archbishop</a> <a href="/wiki/Desmond_Tutu" title="Desmond Tutu">Desmond Tutu</a>, and several other Nobel Peace laureates stayed on Grounds for one week in 1998 while attending the University's historic Nobel Laureates Conference.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Student_housing">Student housing</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Virginia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Student housing">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <table class="metadata plainlinks ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" style=""> 
      <tbody>
       <tr> 
        <td class="mbox-image"><a href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg" class="image"><img alt="[icon]" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" width="20" height="14" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" /></a></td> 
        <td class="mbox-text" style=""><span class="mbox-text-span">This section requires <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=University_of_Virginia&amp;action=edit">expansion</a>. <small><i>(October 2011)</i></small></span></td> 
       </tr> 
      </tbody>
     </table> 
     <p>The housing units that permit families are Copeley Hill Apartments and University Gardens.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> Copeley Hill is within the <a href="/wiki/Albemarle_County_Public_Schools" title="Albemarle County Public Schools">Albemarle County Public Schools</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> Residents of Copeley Hill are zoned to Greer Elementary School,<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> Jouett Middle School,<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Albemarle_High_School" title="Albemarle High School">Albemarle High School</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> University Gardens is within the <a href="/wiki/Charlottesville_City_Schools" title="Charlottesville City Schools" class="mw-redirect">Charlottesville City Schools</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> University Gardens is zoned to Venable Elementary School, Walker Upper Elementary School, Buford Middle School, and <a href="/wiki/Charlottesville_High_School" title="Charlottesville High School">Charlottesville High School</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Admissions">Admissions</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Virginia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Admissions">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="High_preference_among_high_achievers">High preference among high achievers</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Virginia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: High preference among high achievers">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Alderman_Library.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Alderman_Library.JPG/250px-Alderman_Library.JPG" width="250" height="195" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Alderman_Library.JPG/375px-Alderman_Library.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Alderman_Library.JPG/500px-Alderman_Library.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Alderman_Library.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Alderman_Library&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Alderman Library (page does not exist)">Alderman Library</a> includes one of the largest 
        <a href="/wiki/Tibet" title="Tibet">Tibetan</a> collections in the world,
        <sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup> one of many special collections housed beneath its &quot;stacks&quot;.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Admission to the University of Virginia is competitive, with 90.2% of admitted applicants ranking in the top 10% of their high school classes.<sup id="cite_ref-uva-2011stats_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uva-2011stats-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup> A December 2005 <a href="/wiki/National_Bureau_of_Economic_Research" title="National Bureau of Economic Research">National Bureau of Economic Research</a> study of &quot;high-achieving&quot; undergraduate applicants found U.Va., at twentieth overall, to be the most preferred college located in the state of Virginia, and the second-most preferred in the <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">American South</a>, behind <a href="/wiki/Duke_University" title="Duke University">Duke University</a>. The study also revealed the University to be the most preferred <a href="/wiki/Public_university" title="Public university">public university</a> in the entire United States. The stated purpose of the NBER study was to produce a ranking system that &quot;would be difficult for a college to manipulate&quot; by basing it on the actual demonstrated preferences of highly meritorious students.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Admissions_statistics">Admissions statistics</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Virginia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Admissions statistics">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:IMPsecretsociety.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/IMPsecretsociety.jpg/250px-IMPsecretsociety.jpg" width="250" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/IMPsecretsociety.jpg/375px-IMPsecretsociety.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/IMPsecretsociety.jpg/500px-IMPsecretsociety.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:IMPsecretsociety.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The mark of 
        <a href="/wiki/IMP_Society" title="IMP Society">one</a> out of 
        <a href="/wiki/Secret_societies_at_the_University_of_Virginia" title="Secret societies at the University of Virginia">many</a> secret societies active on Grounds at the University
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>For the Class of 2011, the University of Virginia received a record 18,013 applications.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> The University saw increased interest from various groups of students, as applications rose by 13 percent for <a href="/wiki/African_American" title="African American">African American</a> applicants, 20 percent for <a href="/wiki/Asian_American" title="Asian American">Asian Americans</a>, 16 percent for <a href="/wiki/Hispanic_and_Latino_Americans" title="Hispanic and Latino Americans">Hispanic Americans</a>, and 26 percent for <a href="/wiki/International" title="International">international</a> students. The University enrolled 70 more first-years than it did the previous year, as it continued to expand the scope of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Virginia_School_of_Engineering_and_Applied_Science" title="University of Virginia School of Engineering and Applied Science">School of Engineering and Applied Science</a>.</p> 
     <p>Another record was established for the Class of 2012, with 18,776 applications for 3,170 undergraduate spots.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> Applications rose for each of the four undergraduate schools that accept first-year students into their programs: Architecture, Arts &amp; Sciences, Engineering, and Nursing.</p> 
     <p>The Class of 2013 saw a tremendous increase to yet another new record high of 21,511 applications.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> 37% of applicants were accepted.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> The University continued to see interest from an increasingly diverse pool, as applications increased by another 22 percent for African American students, 56 percent for Hispanic students, 50 percent for international students, and 100 percent for Native Americans.</p> 
     <p>The trend of the previous years continued for the Class of 2014. Application numbers rose to 22,516, of which 32% were offered admission.<sup id="cite_ref-uva-2011stats_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uva-2011stats-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup> Average SAT scores (math, critical reading, writing) rose 10 points from the previous year to 1,993 points. Thirty percent of the Class of 2014, or 983 students, identified themselves as members of one or more minority group.</p> 
     <p>Applications continued to increase for the Class of 2016, which was the first time UVA allowed high school students to apply in the fall for <a href="/wiki/Early_action" title="Early action">early action</a>. 11,753 students applied for early action and 3,187 were offered admission.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> Overall applications for both early action and regular decision grew by 17.64% to 28,200, an increase of 4,229 from the year before.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> The average SAT score for admitted students were 1,413 on a 1,600-point scale, and 2,119 on a 2,400-point scale. Ninety-eight percent of the admitted students were in the Top 10% of their high school class.</p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Academics">Academics</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Virginia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Academics">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Madison_Hall,_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4252.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Madison_Hall%2C_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4252.JPG/200px-Madison_Hall%2C_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4252.JPG" width="200" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Madison_Hall%2C_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4252.JPG/300px-Madison_Hall%2C_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4252.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Madison_Hall%2C_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4252.JPG/400px-Madison_Hall%2C_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4252.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Madison_Hall,_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4252.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Madison Hall, located across from the Rotunda, has housed General Studies since 1960.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Degrees from the University of Virginia must be earned academically ? there has never been an <a href="/wiki/Honorary_degree" title="Honorary degree">honorary degree</a> offered.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> The policy was instituted by Thomas Jefferson. When the Virginia Legislature's Committee of Schools and Colleges was reconsidering it in 1845, then-U.Va. professor and future <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">Massachusetts Institute of Technology</a> founder <a href="/wiki/William_Barton_Rogers" title="William Barton Rogers">William Barton Rogers</a> wrote, &quot;[T]he legislators of the University have, we think, wisely made their highest academic honor ? that of Master of Arts of the University of Virginia ? the genuine test of diligent and successful literary training, and, disdaining such literary almsgiving, have firmly barred the door against the demands of spurious merit and noisy popularity.&quot; When MIT was chartered in Boston, Massachusetts, in 1861, Rogers carried the U.Va. policy through to the new institute.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-barton-mit-founding_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-barton-mit-founding-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The University of Virginia places <sup><span style="font-variant:small-caps">#</span></sup>1 among state-supported universities in the United States in the production of <a href="/wiki/Rhodes_Scholarship#Notable_universities" title="Rhodes Scholarship">Rhodes Scholars</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup> The University's 48th Rhodes Scholar was named in 2012.</p> 
     <p>Tuition is lower for both in-state and out-of-state students than at most other top universities. The student composition of the University is such that it was described in a feature article in the 2006 <i>America's Best Colleges</i> edition of <i><a href="/wiki/U.S._News_and_World_Report" title="U.S. News and World Report" class="mw-redirect">U.S. News and World Report</a></i> as being &quot;chock full of academic stars who turn down private schools like <a href="/wiki/Duke_University" title="Duke University">Duke</a>, <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cornell_University" title="Cornell University">Cornell</a> for, they say, a better value.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup> Indeed, in 2008 the Center for College Affordability and Productivity named the University the top value among all national public colleges and universities;<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup> and in 2009, the University was again named the &quot;#1 Best Value&quot; among public universities in the United States in a separate ranking by <i><a href="/wiki/USA_TODAY" title="USA TODAY" class="mw-redirect">USA TODAY</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Princeton_Review" title="Princeton Review" class="mw-redirect">Princeton Review</a>.</i><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rankings_and_recognition">Rankings and recognition</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Virginia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Rankings and recognition">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <table class="infobox vcard" style="width: 22em"> 
      <tbody>
       <tr> 
        <th colspan="2" class="n" style="text-align: center; font-size: 125%"><span class="fn"><a href="/wiki/College_and_university_rankings" title="College and university rankings">University rankings</a></span></th> 
       </tr> 
       <tr> 
        <th colspan="2" style="background-color: lavender; text-align: center">National</th> 
       </tr> 
       <tr> 
        <th><i><a href="/wiki/Academic_Ranking_of_World_Universities" title="Academic Ranking of World Universities">ARWU</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-Rankings_ARWU_NU_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rankings_ARWU_NU-78"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup></th> 
        <td>56</td> 
       </tr> 
       <tr> 
        <th><i><a href="/wiki/Forbes" title="Forbes">Forbes</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-Rankings_Forbes_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rankings_Forbes-79"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup></th> 
        <td>32</td> 
       </tr> 
       <tr> 
        <th><i><a href="/wiki/U.S._News_%26_World_Report" title="U.S. News &amp; World Report">U.S. News &amp; World Report</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-Rankings_USNWR_NU_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rankings_USNWR_NU-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup></th> 
        <td>24</td> 
       </tr>
